Sukut as Bayan: Applications and the Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Applying the rule of sukut as bayan and addressing the ikhtilaf with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of sukut to the silence of a shafi' and a master, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection to the master's silence.

Core Idea
Silence can constitute permission by necessity and custom, even if not explicit.

The Hanafi school extends the concept of silence as bayan to practical scenarios where silence must be interpreted as consent to avoid harm. This lesson examines two key applications: the silence of a pre-emptor (shafi') and the silence of a master seeing his slave trade. The lesson also addresses the Shafi'i objection that silence is ambiguous and the Hanafi response rooted in custom and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shafi' scenario
The shafi' (pre-emptor) remains silent after learning of the sale. If his silence is not considered a waiver, the buyer is harmed by uncertainty, and the seller may lose potential buyers.
Silence is interpreted as waiver to prevent harm.
2
Understand the master's scenario
A master sees his slave engaging in trade and remains silent. The Hanafi position is that this silence is permission for the slave to trade, to protect third parties who deal with the slave from deception.
Necessity (darurah) and custom ('urf) are key.
3
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Al-Shafi'i argues that the master's silence could be due to anger or disregard, not consent, and since it is ambiguous (muhtamal), it cannot serve as a proof.
This objection highlights a methodological difference.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response concedes that silence is theoretically ambiguous but asserts that dominant custom (ghalib al-'urf) gives weight to interpreting it as consent, because people generally do not remain silent about disapproved actions in such contexts.
Custom (urf) is used to resolve ambiguity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical outcome: In Hanafi law, the slave's trade is valid and binds the master; in Shafi'i law, the trade would be invalid unless explicit permission is proven.
This difference affects real transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيان
أقسام البيان
وفي موضع الحاجة إلى البيان يدل على البيان مثل سكوت الصحابة عن تقويم منفعة البدن في ولد المغرور، ومنه ما يثبت ضرورة دفع الغرور مثل سكوت الشفيع وسكوت المولى حين يرى عبده يبيع ويشتري ومنه ما يثبت بضرورة كثرة الكلام، مثل قول علمائنا له فيمن قال له علي مائة ودرهم أو مائة وقفيز حنطة، إن العطف وفعل في زمانه، حتى لم يتكلّم ولم يغيّر، وأصر الفاعل على فعله، يدلّ على كون ذلك الأمر حقاً، وذلك مثل ما شاهد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من البيوع والمعاملات التي كان الناس يتعاملونها فيما بينهم من المأكل والمشرب والملابس التي كانوا يواظبون عليها، فسكت عنها عليه السلام وأقرهم عليها، ولم ينكر عليهم مع العلم والقدرة، فكان سكوته بياناً أن تلك الأمور داخلة في المعروف وخارجة عن المنكر؛ إذ ليس من شأن النبي أن يترك الناس على أمر منكر، وقد قال الله تعالى في حقه: ﴿يَأْمُرُهُمْ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَيَنْهَاهُمْ عَنِ الْمُنْكَرِ (الأعراف:١٥٧)، وكذا السكوت. ولد المغرور : المغرور رجل يطأ امرأة معتمداً على ملك اليمين فتلد، فيظهر بعد ذلك أنها للغير أو على النكاح بأن قالت تلك المرأة: أنا حرة، فتلد فيثبت أنها مملوكة للغير، ويقال له المغرور؛ لأنه أغرته تلك المرأة، فولد المغرور حرّ، ويلزم عليه أداء قيمة ولده لصاحب الأمة، وأما منافع بدنه التي حصلت لأبيه فغير مقومة، أي لا يجب أداء قيمتها على أبيه، وذلك لأن امرأة أبقت وتزوجت رجلا من بني عذرة فولدت أولاداً، ثم جاء مولاها ورفع تلك القضية على عمر له، فقضى بها لمولاها وقضى على الأب أن يفدي عن أولادها ويأخذهم بالقيمة وسكت عن ضمان منافعها ومنافع ولدها، وكان ذلك بمحضر من الصحابة، فسكوتهم عند ذلك يدلّ على البيان بأنهم رضوا بذلك، وقد روى هذه القصة رزين عن مالك، وذكر مالك في مؤطاه هذه القصة بعض الأشياء. ومنه: أي من بيان الضرورة وهذا وجه ثالث منه. دفع الغرور عن الناس، والغرور حرام. سكوت الشفيع عن طلب الشفعة بعد العلم بالبيع، فإنه إذا لم يجعل سكوته إسقاط الشفعة يتضرّر به المشتري؛ لأنه يحبس عن تصرفاته في المبيع لأجل خوف الشفيع، وكذا يتضرّر به البائع؛ لأنه ربما لا يشتريه رجل خوفاً من الشفيع فيتضرر. وسكوت المولى إلخ : فإن سكوته حين ذلك بيان؛ لأنه أذن له في التجارة دفعاً للغرور عن الناس، وقال الشافعي لا يكون سكوته إذناً لاحتمال أنه سكت للغيظ وقلة المبالاة إلى تصرفه لعلمه بأنه محجور، والمحتمل لا يكون حجة. قلنا: سكوته وإن كان محتملا لكن الغالب في العرف ترجيح جانب الرضاء للعادة الجارية بين الناس.
Chapter of Bayan. Sections of Bayan. And in the place of need for Bayan, what indicates Bayan is like the silence of the Companions regarding evaluating the benefit of the body in the child of al-maghrur. And from it is what is proven by necessity of repelling deception, like the silence of the shafi' and the silence of the master when he sees his slave buying and selling. And from it is what is proven by necessity of abundance of speech, like the saying of our scholars to him concerning one who said to him 'I owe one hundred and a dirham' or 'one hundred and a qafiz of wheat': indeed the conjunction... and he acted in his time such that he did not speak and did not change, and the doer persisted in his action, indicating that that matter is true. That is like what the Prophet observed of sales and transactions that people conducted among themselves regarding food, drink, and clothing that they constantly engaged in. He remained silent about them and confirmed them, and he did not object despite knowledge and ability. So his silence was a declaration that those matters are included in the known good and excluded from the forbidden, for it is not the practice of the Prophet to leave people upon a forbidden matter. And Allah the Exalted said regarding him: 'He commands them to the good and forbids them from the evil' (al-A'raf 157), and likewise is silence. The child of al-maghrur: al-maghrur is a man who has intercourse with a woman relying on ownership by oath, and she gives birth, then it appears that she belongs to another. Or it occurs due to marriage such that the woman says 'I am free' and she gives birth, then it is established that she is owned by another. He is called al-maghrur because that woman deceived him. So the child of al-maghrur is free, and he is obligated to pay the value of his child to the owner of the slave-woman. As for the benefits of his body that accrued to his father, they are not evaluated, meaning he is not required to pay their value to the father. This is because a slave-woman fled and married a man from Banu 'Udhra and bore children. Then her master came and raised that case to 'Umar, so he ruled in favor of her master and ruled that the father should ransom her children and take them by paying value, and he remained silent about liability for her benefits and the benefits of her child. That occurred in the presence of the Companions, so their silence at that indicates a declaration that they approved that. This story was narrated by Razin from Malik, and Malik mentioned in his Muwatta' some things of this story. And from it: i.e., from the declaration by necessity, and this is the third aspect of it: repelling deception from people, and deception is prohibited. The silence of the shafi' from demanding pre-emption after knowledge of the sale: for if his silence is not made a waiver of pre-emption, the buyer would be harmed because he is prevented from dealing with the sold property due to fear of the shafi', and likewise the seller would be harmed because a man might not buy out of fear of the shafi'. And the silence of the master, etc.: for his silence at that time is a declaration because he permitted him to trade to repel deception from people. Al-Shafi'i said: his silence is not permission because it is possible that he remained silent out of anger and lack of concern for his transaction due to his knowledge that he is interdicted, and the possible cannot be a proof. We said: his silence, although it is possible, but the dominant in custom is giving preponderance to the side of satisfaction due to the established practice among people.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a definition and demonstration of 'sukut' (silence) as a form of bayan (declaration) in Hanafi usul. The author lists three instances where silence functions as a declaration of permissibility or consent. First, the silence of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding common transactions witnessed during his time proves that those practices are permissible, because it is impossible for him to remain silent upon falsehood. This is supported by the Quranic verse from al-A'raf 157. Second, the silence of the Companions when 'Umar ruled on the case of the child of al-maghrur—specifically, not requiring compensation for the usufruct of the child—indicates their tacit approval and thus serves as a precedent. Third, the silence of the shafi' (pre-emptor) after knowledge of the sale is considered a waiver of the right of pre-emption by necessity to avoid harm to the buyer and seller. Fourth, the silence of a master when he sees his slave engaging in trade is considered permission to trade, to avoid harming third parties who deal with the slave. The author then addresses the objection of Imam al-Shafi'i, who argues that such silence is ambiguous and cannot be a proof. The Hanafi response is that custom and general practice give greater weight to interpreting such silence as consent. This illustrates the Hanafi reliance on 'urf (custom) and darurah (necessity) as evidentiary sources.

Key Terms
السكوت (al-sukut): Refraining from speech or action in the presence of a matter, which can constitute a declaration of approval or permissibility.
بيان الضرورة (bayan al-darurah): A type of declaration necessitated by circumstances to avoid harm or uncertainty.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption in property sales.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Hanafi school interpret a master's silence when seeing his slave trade as permission?
A) Because the master explicitly told the slave to trade
B) Because silence is always a form of consent in Hanafi law
C) Because the dominant custom and the necessity to repel deception support that interpretation
D) Because the slave forced the master to remain silent

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, custom and necessity can render silence a legally effective declaration of permission, even when the silence is theoretically ambiguous.

The Default Ruling of the Prophet's Actions: Iqtida' and Ibaha
Defines the principle of iqtida' and the default ruling of ibaha

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the Prophet's actions are to be followed (iqtida') by default, and identify that an unknown manner defaults to ibaha.

Core Idea
Follow the Prophet's actions unless proof shows they were specific to him.

How do we know whether an action of the Prophet is obligatory, recommended, or simply permissible for us? The Hanafi school lays out a clear principle: we are to follow his actions (iqtida') unless there is evidence that the action was specific to him. This lesson unpacks that principle and shows that when the manner of an action is unknown, we default to the lowest level—permissibility (ibaha).

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule for following the Prophet's actions
The text states that following (iqtida') is the foundation, so we adhere to it until evidence shows specificity (khususiyya).
This is a key Hanafi usul principle: al-asl al-ittiba'.
2
Understand the three levels of the Prophet's actions
The Prophet's actions can be wajib, mandub, or mubah. If the specific level is known, we follow accordingly.
These correspond to the legal categories of his actions.
3
Apply the default when the manner is unknown
If we do not know which level the Prophet performed an action, we take the lowest level (adna manazil), which is ibaha (permissibility).
This is based on the principle that the default in doubtful cases is permission.
4
Recognize the exception: slips and specific actions
Actions that are slips (zalla) or known to be specific to the Prophet (khususiyya) are not subject to iqtida'.
The text mentions that slips are not from the chapter of following.
5
Connect the principle to the Quranic evidence
The verse 'Indeed in the Messenger of God you have a good example' (Quran 33:21) establishes the obligation of following his example.
This verse is the primary dalil for iqtida'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أفعال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم
لكنه ليس من هذا الباب في شيء؛ لأنه لا يصلح للاقتداء، ويخلو عن الاقتران ببيان
أي القسم الآخر أي من باب الاقتداء
أنه ،زلة واختلف في سائر أفعاله عليه السلام والصحيح ما قاله الجصاص: إن ما
علمنا
اقتداء
هو أبو بكر الرازي
أفعال الرسول و واقعاً على جهة يقتدى به في إيقاعه على تلك الجهة، من مفعول لقوله: "وعلمنا" وما لم نعلمه على أي جهة فعله فلنا فعله على أدنى منازل أفعاله، وهو الإباحة؛ لأن
الاتباع أصل، فوجب التمسك به حتى يقوم دليل خصوصه به.
به
ببيان إلخ : وهذا البيان قد يكون من جهة الفاعل كقوله تعالى (إخباراً عن موسى عليه حين وكز القبطي فمات): هَذَا مِنْ عَمَل الشَّيْطَانِ ﴾ (القصص: ١٥)، وقد يكون من الله تعالى كما قال عزّ وجلّ في آدم عليها حين أكل الشجرة من الزلة: ﴿وَعَصَى آدَمُ رَبَّهُ فَعَوَى (طه: ۱۲۱). فإن قلت: لما ذكر المصنف أفعاله مما يقتدى به من الأقسام الأربعة ومما لا يقتدي به كالزلة كان عليه أن يذكر الحرام والمكروه؛ لأنهما أيضاً مما لا يقتدى به. قلتُ: الحرام والمكروه لا يوجدان في أفعاله لله و إن كانا مما لا يقتدى به؛ لأن الأنبياء معصومون من الكبائر عند جمهور المسلمين، ومن الصغائر عندنا خلافاً لبعض الأشاعرة، والحرام والمكروه داخلان تحت الكبائر والصغائر. سائر أفعاله عليه السلام التي لم تصدر عنه سهواً ولم تكن له طبعاً كأكل وشرب ولم تكن مخصوصةً كإباحة الزيادة على الأربعة في النكاح، فقال البعض كأبي بكر الدقاق والغزالي: يجب التوقف حتى يتبين أنه عليه على أي جهة فعل ذلك من الإباحة والندب والوجوب، وقال بعضهم كمالك وأبي العباس وابن شريح: يجب الاتباع مطلقا ما لم يقم دليل المنع، وأشار المصنف إلى ما هو المختار عنده. على جهة أي على صفة من الإباحة أو الندب أو الوجوب على تلك الجهة فما كان مباحاً له يكون مباحاً لنا، وما كان مندوباً له يكون مندوباً لنا، وما كان واجباً عليه يكون واجباً علينا أي جهة فعله أي من جهة الإباحة أو الندب أو الوجوب. وهو الإباحة وذلك لأنه لم يفعل حراماً أو مكروهاً فلا محالة يكون مباحاً فنقتدي بفعله عليه السلام لأن الاتباع أصل كما قال تعالى: "لقد كان لكم في رسول الله أسوة حسنة" فهذا تصريح على وجوب التأسي بأفعاله عليه السلام به أي بالأصل أو بفعله حتى يقوم إلخ: أي ما لم يقم دليل على أن هذا الفعل خاص له هلال الهلال الزمنا اتباعه؛ لكونه علي إمام الهدى.
The actions of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace. But it is not part of this chapter at all; because it is not suitable for following, and it is devoid of conjunction with explanation. That is, the other category, that is, from the chapter of following. That it is a slip, and they disagreed regarding the rest of his actions, peace be upon him, and the correct is what al-Jassas said: That which we know the actions of the Messenger occurred in a manner that is followed in performing it in that manner, from the object of His saying: 'And we know' and what we do not know in which manner he did it, we may do it in the lowest of the levels of his actions, which is permissibility; because following is the foundation, so it is necessary to adhere to it until evidence arises of its specificity to him. With explanation, etc.: And this explanation may be from the doer, like His saying (informing about Moses, peace be upon him, when he struck the Copt and he died): 'This is from the work of Satan' (Qasas 28:15), and it may be from God Almighty as He said, Mighty and Majestic, regarding Adam, peace be upon him, when he ate from the tree of the slip: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and erred' (Taha 20:121). If you say: When the author mentioned his actions that are followed from the four categories and what is not followed like the slip, he should have mentioned the forbidden and the disliked; because they too are not followed. I say: The forbidden and the disliked are not found in his actions because the prophets are infallible from major sins according to the majority of Muslims, and from minor sins according to us contrary to some Ash'aris, and the forbidden and disliked are included under major and minor sins. The rest of his actions, peace be upon him, that did not occur from him forgetfully and were not natural for him like eating and drinking, and were not specific like permitting increase over four in marriage. Some, like Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq and al-Ghazali, said: It is necessary to pause until it becomes clear in which manner he did it from permissibility, recommendation, or obligation. And some, like Malik, Abu al-Abbas, and Ibn Shurayh, said: It is necessary to follow absolutely as long as no evidence of prohibition exists. And the author indicated what is chosen according to him. 'In a manner' i.e., in a description of permissibility, recommendation, or obligation. In that manner, what was permissible for him is permissible for us, what was recommended for him is recommended for us, and what was obligatory for him is obligatory for us. 'In which manner he did it' i.e., from the perspective of permissibility, recommendation, or obligation. And it is permissibility because he did not do something forbidden or disliked, so inevitably it is permissible. So we follow his action, peace be upon him, because following is the foundation, as He said: 'Indeed in the Messenger of God you have a good example.' This is a declaration of the obligation of taking example by his actions, peace be upon him. 'By it' i.e., by the foundation or by his action, 'until there exists' etc.: i.e., as long as no evidence exists that this action is specific to him.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the legal status of the Prophet's actions (af'al al-Rasul) and whether they are binding for the ummah. The author begins by stating that actions which are not suitable for following (like slips) are not part of this chapter. He then references al-Jassas's position: we follow actions that we know were performed in a manner intended for emulation; for actions where the manner is unknown, we default to the lowest level, which is permissibility (ibaha), because adherence to the Prophet's practice is the foundation until proof of specificity arises. The commentary further explains that explanations of such actions can come from the doer (like Prophet Moses attributing an act to Satan) or from God (like Adam's act being called disobedience). The author addresses an objection: why didn't the author mention haram and makruh? The response is that prophets are infallible from major sins and, according to the Hanafis, from minor sins as well, so such categories do not apply. For actions that are not forgetful, natural, or specific, scholars differed: some (like al-Daqqaq and al-Ghazali) require pause until the manner is clarified, while others (like Malik) require absolute following unless evidence of prohibition. The author indicates the preferred view: the Prophet's actions are to be followed in their apparent manner (ibaha, nadb, wajib) based on the Quranic command to take him as a good example. Thus, the default is permissibility for us, but if evidence shows it was a specific obligation or recommendation, we follow that.

Key Terms
الاقتداء (al-iqtida'): Following the Prophet's actions as a legal model for the ummah
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Legal permissibility; the default level for actions of the Prophet whose manner is unknown
الزلة (al-zalla): A slip or unintentional error by a prophet, not subject to iqtida'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the default ruling for an action of the Prophet whose manner of performance is unknown?
A) Obligatory (wajib)
B) Recommended (mandub)
C) Permissible (mubah)
D) Forbidden (haram)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle is that the Prophet's actions are followed by default; when the manner is unknown, they are permissible (mubah) until evidence shows otherwise.

The Ikhtilaf on Following the Prophet's Actions of Unknown Manner
Presents the scholarly disagreement on following the Prophet's actions of unknown manner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main positions on whether to follow the Prophet's actions when the manner is unknown, and explain the Hanafi preferred view.

Core Idea
When the manner is unknown, some pause (tawaqquf), others follow absolutely; the Hanafis choose a middle path.

The passage presents a significant disagreement among scholars about how to treat the Prophet's actions when we do not know whether he did them as obligatory, recommended, or permissible. Some argued that we must suspend judgment until the manner becomes known; others insisted that we follow the action absolutely unless there is proof of prohibition. The author of this usul text indicates the chosen position, which aligns with the Hanafi approach. This lesson explores that ikhtilaf and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of actions in dispute
The actions in question are those that are not slips (zalla), not natural acts (like eating/drinking), and not known to be specific to the Prophet (khususiyya).
These are the 'rest of his actions' that are potential models.
2
State the first position: tawaqquf (pause)
Al-Daqqaq and al-Ghazali (among others) held that we must wait until evidence clarifies whether the action was wajib, mandub, or mubah before we can follow it in that manner.
This is a cautious approach avoiding assuming a level without proof.
3
State the second position: absolute following
Malik, Abu al-Abbas, and Ibn Shurayh held that we must follow the action absolutely (mutlaqan) as long as no clear evidence of prohibition exists.
This view emphasizes the general command to follow the Prophet.
4
Identify the Hanafi selected view
The author indicates that the chosen view (al-mukhtar) is the one attributed to al-Jassas: when the manner is known, we follow accordingly; when unknown, we default to ibaha (permissibility), which is the lowest level.
This is a middle path that avoids both the extreme of pause and the extreme of absolute obligation.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference affects whether an action becomes immediately permissible (mufta bihi for Hanafis) or remains uncertain (tawaqquf) or becomes binding (absolute following) until proven otherwise.
The Hanafi position allows practical application without undue hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The actions of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace. But it is not part of this chapter at all; because it is not suitable for following, and it is devoid of conjunction with explanation. That is, the other category, that is, from the chapter of following. That it is a slip, and they disagreed regarding the rest of his actions, peace be upon him, and the correct is what al-Jassas said: That which we know the actions of the Messenger occurred in a manner that is followed in performing it in that manner, from the object of His saying: 'And we know' and what we do not know in which manner he did it, we may do it in the lowest of the levels of his actions, which is permissibility; because following is the foundation, so it is necessary to adhere to it until evidence arises of its specificity to him. With explanation, etc.: And this explanation may be from the doer, like His saying (informing about Moses, peace be upon him, when he struck the Copt and he died): 'This is from the work of Satan' (Qasas 28:15), and it may be from God Almighty as He said, Mighty and Majestic, regarding Adam, peace be upon him, when he ate from the tree of the slip: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and erred' (Taha 20:121). If you say: When the author mentioned his actions that are followed from the four categories and what is not followed like the slip, he should have mentioned the forbidden and the disliked; because they too are not followed. I say: The forbidden and the disliked are not found in his actions because the prophets are infallible from major sins according to the majority of Muslims, and from minor sins according to us contrary to some Ash'aris, and the forbidden and disliked are included under major and minor sins. The rest of his actions, peace be upon him, that did not occur from him forgetfully and were not natural for him like eating and drinking, and were not specific like permitting increase over four in marriage. Some, like Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq and al-Ghazali, said: It is necessary to pause until it becomes clear in which manner he did it from permissibility, recommendation, or obligation. And some, like Malik, Abu al-Abbas, and Ibn Shurayh, said: It is necessary to follow absolutely as long as no evidence of prohibition exists. And the author indicated what is chosen according to him. 'In a manner' i.e., in a description of permissibility, recommendation, or obligation. In that manner, what was permissible for him is permissible for us, what was recommended for him is recommended for us, and what was obligatory for him is obligatory for us. 'In which manner he did it' i.e., from the perspective of permissibility, recommendation, or obligation. And it is permissibility because he did not do something forbidden or disliked, so inevitably it is permissible. So we follow his action, peace be upon him, because following is the foundation, as He said: 'Indeed in the Messenger of God you have a good example.' This is a declaration of the obligation of taking example by his actions, peace be upon him. 'By it' i.e., by the foundation or by his action, 'until there exists' etc.: i.e., as long as no evidence exists that this action is specific to him.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the legal status of the Prophet's actions (af'al al-Rasul) and whether they are binding for the ummah. The author begins by stating that actions which are not suitable for following (like slips) are not part of this chapter. He then references al-Jassas's position: we follow actions that we know were performed in a manner intended for emulation; for actions where the manner is unknown, we default to the lowest level, which is permissibility (ibaha), because adherence to the Prophet's practice is the foundation until proof of specificity arises. The commentary further explains that explanations of such actions can come from the doer (like Prophet Moses attributing an act to Satan) or from God (like Adam's act being called disobedience). The author addresses an objection: why didn't the author mention haram and makruh? The response is that prophets are infallible from major sins and, according to the Hanafis, from minor sins as well, so such categories do not apply. For actions that are not forgetful, natural, or specific, scholars differed: some (like al-Daqqaq and al-Ghazali) require pause until the manner is clarified, while others (like Malik) require absolute following unless evidence of prohibition. The author indicates the preferred view: the Prophet's actions are to be followed in their apparent manner (ibaha, nadb, wajib) based on the Quranic command to take him as a good example. Thus, the default is permissibility for us, but if evidence shows it was a specific obligation or recommendation, we follow that.

Key Terms
التوقف (al-tawaqquf): Suspending judgment or action until the legal status of the Prophet's action becomes clear
الاتباع مطلقاً (al-ittiba' mutlaqan): Absolute following of the Prophet's action unless there is evidence of prohibition
المختار (al-mukhtar): The chosen view among the scholars, indicated by the author

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following scholars advocated for 'tawaqquf' (pausing) when the manner of a Prophetic action is unknown?
A) Abu Bakr al-Daqqaq and al-Ghazali
B) Malik and Abu al-Abbas
C) Al-Jassas and the author
D) Ibn Shurayh

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes itself by defaulting to ibaha for unknown Prophetic actions, avoiding both the tawaqquf and absolute following extremes.

The Prophet's Ijtihad as Definitive Proof
usul rule on prophetic ijtihad and its authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that the Prophet's confirmed ijtihad yields a definitive proof (qat‘ī) and distinguish it from ordinary mujtahids' ijtihad.

Core Idea
When the Prophet's ijtihad is not corrected by revelation, it becomes a definitive proof binding all.

This passage addresses a key usul question: Did the Prophet ﷺ use ijtihad? While some schools deny it, the Hanafi position affirms it under specific conditions. The passage emphasizes that the Prophet's ijtihad, once confirmed by the absence of revelatory correction, carries the weight of a definitive proof (qat‘ī), unlike the probabilistic ijtihad of other scholars. This has profound implications for how we treat rulings derived from the Prophet's practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of dispute
Did the Prophet engage in ijtihad when revelation was silent? Note the different scholarly opinions.
The passage states 'واختلفوا في هذا الفصل' indicating a known ikhtilaf.
2
State the Hanafi position
The correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ ʿindanā) is that he used ijtihad when revelation was delayed and he was tested with a matter.
This is the foundation of the rule.
3
Understand the mechanism of confirmation
If the Prophet's ijtihad was in error, revelation would correct it. The absence of correction indicates correctness and divine approval.
The phrase 'وَلا يُقَرُّ عَلَى الْخَطَأِ' means he is not confirmed upon error.
4
Grasp the legal consequence
Once confirmed, his ijtihad becomes a definitive indication (dalālah qāṭiʿah) of the ruling, unlike the ijtihad of other mujtahids which remains probabilistic.
This is the key analytical outcome.
5
Distinguish from ordinary ijtihad and ilhām
The Prophet's ijtihad is likened to inspiration (ilhām) in terms of its conclusiveness for him, but this quality is unique to him.
For others, ilhām is not a proof in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أفعال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بيان طريقة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في إظهار الأحكام] ويتصل بالسنن بيان طريقة رسول الله الله في إظهار أحكام الشرع بالاجتهاد، واختلفوا في هذا الفصل، والصحيح عندنا أنه كان يعمل بالاجتهاد إذا انقطع طمعه عن الوحى فيما ابتلي به وكان لايقرّ على الخطأ، فإذا أقرّ على شيء من ذلك مثل سائر المجتهدين [ كان] دلالةً قاطعةً على الحكم، بخلاف ما يكون على الحكم، بخلاف ما يكون من غيره من البيان بالرأي، وهو على إصابته من سائر المجتهدين نظير الإلهام، فإنه حجة قاطعة في حقه وإن لم يكن في حق غيره بهذه الصفة. أي إلهامه عليا
Acts of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. Explanation of the method of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, in manifesting rulings. And connected to the Sunnahs is the explanation of the method of the Messenger of Allah in manifesting the rulings of the Shariah through ijtihad. They differed in this section, and the correct [view] according to us is that he used to act by ijtihad when his hope of revelation was cut off in matters he was tested with, and he would not be confirmed upon error. If he was confirmed upon something of that like other mujtahids, it is a definitive indication of the ruling, unlike what comes from others of explanation by opinion. And it is, regarding his accuracy, like inspiration among other mujtahids, for it is a conclusive proof in his right, even if it is not in the right of others with this quality.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the nature of the Prophet's ijtihad (independent reasoning) and its authority. The author states that the correct view in our Hanafi madhhab is that the Prophet ﷺ did engage in ijtihad when revelation was not forthcoming on a particular matter. He would wait for revelation for a period (some say three days, or until fear of missing the obligation), and if no revelation came, he would form his own opinion. Importantly, if he made an error in his ijtihad, Allah would send a revelation to correct it. Thus, if no correction came and his heart settled on a ruling, that ruling is considered a definitive proof (qati' dalalah) and binding, unlike the ijtihad of other scholars which is probabilistic. This is likened to inspiration (ilham) for the Prophet—his ijtihad is like a definitive proof for him, though for others it is not. This establishes that once the Prophet's ijtihad is confirmed by the absence of revelatory correction, it becomes part of the Shariah with full authority.

Key Terms
اجتهاد (ijtihād): Independent legal reasoning by a qualified jurist to derive a ruling from the sources.
إلهام (ilhām): Inspiration from Allah that occurs to the Prophet or saints; for the Prophet it is a definitive proof.
دلالة قاطعة (dalālah qāṭiʿah): A definitive indication that yields certainty, not open to interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what happens if the Prophet's ijtihad is followed by no revelation correcting it?
A) It is considered a probabilistic opinion like any mujtahid’s.
B) It becomes a definitive proof (dhalālah qāṭiʿah) binding on all.
C) It must be abandoned because the Prophet only relied on revelation.
D) It is valid only for that specific incident but not for future cases.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's ijtihad, when confirmed by the absence of revelatory correction, yields a definitive proof (qat‘ī) that is binding on all Muslims, distinguishing it from the probabilistic ijtihad of other scholars.

Two Types of Iqamat al-Shay' Maqam Ghayrihi
Explaining the two types of iqamat al-shay' maqam ghayrihi with examples and a critical note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of iqamat al-shay' maqam ghayrihi and apply them to identify when a cause or evidence replaces the real effective factor in deriving rulings.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a cause (sabab) is placed in place of its effect, while evidence (dalil) is placed in place of the evident, even if it lacks causal effect.

The concept of 'establishing something in place of another' (iqamat al-shay' maqam ghayrihi) is a key principle in Hanafi usul. It explains how the law uses observable factors (like travel or disease) as effective causes ('illal) even though the real underlying factor (like hardship or harm) is internal and variable. This lesson unpacks two distinct types: the first where a motivational cause stands in for its internal effect, and the second where evidence stands in for the internal state it indicates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the passage carefully and pick out the two categories of 'iqamat al-shay' maqam ghayrihi' that are discussed.
The author explicitly lists them: the first is establishing the sabab in place of the musabbab, the second is establishing the dalil in place of the madlul.
2
Analyze the first type (cause replacing effect)
Examine the example of travel being established in place of hardship. Note how the real cause (hardship) is internal and cannot be measured, so travel (the usual cause) is treated as the effective factor.
The Quranic verse 2:185 is cited to support that Allah intends ease, making travel a license.
3
Analyze the second type (evidence replacing evident)
Examine the examples: the report about love in divorce, and the state of purity (tuhr) as evidence for need for divorce. Understand how the evidence (declaration of love or purity) stands in for the internal state (love or need).
The author critiques the tuhr example as weak, offering a better formulation.
4
Understand the critique
Read the critical note (أقول فيه وهن). Identify why the author considers the tuhr example problematic and what the correct evidence should be.
Tuhr itself is not evidence; rather, the act of initiating divorce in tuhr is evidence of need.
5
Distinguish between cause and evidence
Compare the two types. Note the difference in effect: a cause always has some effect, while evidence may only indicate without causal impact.
This distinction is crucial for proper istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اسما: لأنها تضاف إليه في الشرع، يقال: "القصر رخصة للسفر". وحكما لأنها تثبت بنفس السفر متصلاً به. هي المشقة دون السفر كما أشار إليه الله تعالى: ﴿يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ﴾ (البقرة : ١٨٥). لكن السبب: أي سبب المشقة وهو السفر غالبا. أقيم مقامها: أي مقام المشقة تيسيراً على العباد، ولأنها أمر باطن يتفاوت أحوال الناس فيه، فلا يمكن الوقوف على حقيقتها، فأقام السفر مقامها؛ لأنه سبب لها في الغالب، وهذا مثال للعلة اسما وحكماً لا معنى، ولما بلغ كلام الشيخ إلى إقامة الشيء مقام غيره شرع في بيانه وقال: وإقامة الشيء إلخ. تعريف الشرط كما في السفر والمرض، والثاني إقامة الدليل مقام المدلول كما في الخبر عن المحبّة أقيم مقام المحبة في قوله: إن أحببتني فأنتِ طالق، وكما في الطهر أقيم مقام الحاجة في إباحة الطلاق.. تعريف الشرط] وأما الشرط فهو في الشريعة عبارة عما يضاف إليه الحكم وجوداً عنده لا وجوباً به، وفي اللغة: العلامة السفر والمرض فالسفر سبب داع إلى المشقة، ولما كان الاطلاع عليها متعذراً أقيم مقام المشقة، وجعل علة للرخصة اسماً وحكماً، وكذلك المرض سبب داع إلى التلف، وازدياد المرض الذي هو موجب حقيقي للرخصة لكن لما كان ذلك أمراً باطنا سقط اعتباره في إضافة الحكم إليه وأقيم المرض مقامه، وصار الحكم متعلقا به. الخبر عن المحبة إلخ ذلك الخبر الذي هو دليل على المحبة التي تكون في القلب ولا يمكن الاطلاع عليها بغير الاطلاع الدال على ما في القلب، كما قيل: "جعل الكلام على الفؤاد دليلاً". فأنت طالق فقالت أحبك، فيقع الطلاق الطهر : الخالي عن الجماع، وهو دليل على حاجة الطلاق. إباحة الطلاق يعني إن الطلاق أمر ممنوع لما فيه من قطع النكاح المسنون، إلا أنه شرع ضرورة إنه قد يحتاج إليه عند العجز عن إقامة حقوق النكاح، والحاجة أمر باطن لا يوقف عليه فأقيم دليلها وهو زمان يتجدّد فيه الرغبة وهو الطهر الخالي عن الجماع مقام الحاجة تيسيراً. أقول فيه وهن؛ لأن الطهر نفسه ليس دليل الحاجة كما لا يخفى والأولى أن يقال: إن دليل الحاجة هو الإقدام على الطلاق في الطهر؛ لأنه زمان يرغب الوطي فيه، فإذا أراد الطلاق فيه فيعلم أن له حاجة إلى الطلاق المانع عن الوطي، والفرق بين السبب لا يخلو عن تأثير له في السبب والدليل قد يخلو عن ذلك، فتكون فائدته العلم بالمدلول لا غير. اعلم أن المصنف اقتصر على بعض الأمثلة؛ لأن كثيراً من المسائل الاختلافية تبتني عليه، ولم يأت بالامثلة لجميع أصناف العلل.
Nominally: because it is attributed to it in the law; it is said: 'Shortening prayer is a license for travel.' Legally: because it is established by travel itself, connected to it. It is hardship, not travel, as Allah indicated: 'Allah intends ease for you and does not intend hardship.' (al-Baqara 185). But the cause: i.e., the cause of hardship, which is travel usually, is placed in its position: i.e., the position of hardship, to facilitate for the servants, and because it is an internal matter varying among people, so its reality cannot be known, so travel is placed in its position because it is generally its cause. This is an example of an 'illa nominally and legally, not in meaning. When the Sheikh's speech reached the topic of establishing something in place of another, he began its explanation and said: 'Establishing something in place of another, etc.' Definition of condition as in travel and illness. The second type: establishing the evidence in place of the evident, as in the report about love: it is established in place of love in his statement: 'If you love me, you are divorced,' and as in the state of purity (tuhr) established in place of need for the permissibility of divorce. Definition of condition: In the Shari'a, it is an expression for that to which the ruling is attributed in terms of existence with it, not obligation by it. In language: sign. Travel and illness: travel is a cause inciting hardship, and since knowledge of it is impossible, it is established in place of hardship and made an 'illa for license nominally and legally. Likewise, illness is a cause inciting harm and increase of illness, which is the real cause for license, but since that is an internal matter, its consideration in attributing the ruling to it falls away, and illness is established in its place, and the ruling becomes attached to it. The report about love, etc., that report is evidence of love which is in the heart and cannot be known except by the evidence indicating what is in the heart, as it is said: 'Speech is made a sign of the heart.' So 'You are divorced' and she says 'I love you', then divorce occurs. Tuhr: free from intercourse, is evidence of the need for divorce. Permissibility of divorce means that divorce is prohibited because it cuts the recommended marriage, but it is legislated out of necessity, for one may need it when unable to uphold the rights of marriage. Need is an internal matter not known, so its evidence, which is a time when desire renews, i.e., purity free from intercourse, is established in place of need for facilitation. I say: there is weakness in this, because tuhr itself is not evidence of need, as is not hidden, and better is to say that the evidence of need is the act of initiating divorce in tuhr, because it is a time when one desires intercourse, so if one desires divorce in it, it is known that he has need for divorce which prevents intercourse. The difference between cause and evidence: a cause is not devoid of effect, whereas evidence may be devoid of effect; its benefit is only knowledge of the signified. Know that the author limited himself to some examples, because many disputed issues are based on it, and he did not bring examples for all categories of 'illa.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage elaborates the two types of 'iqamat al-shay' maqam ghayrihi' in usul. The first type is establishing the motivating cause (sabab da'i) in place of the motivated (mad'uw). The example is travel being treated as the 'illa for license (rukhsa) even though the real effective factor is hardship (mashaqqa). The Quranic verse (2:185) sanctions this. The second type is establishing evidence (dalil) in place of the evident (madlul), as in the husband's statement 'If you love me, you are divorced'—the wife's declaration of love (evidence) is taken as proof of love (the internal state), and divorce occurs. Another example is using the state of purity (tuhr, i.e., not menstruating and not having intercourse) as evidence of need for divorce, as divorce is generally discouraged but permitted when needed. The passage includes a critical note (أقول فيه وهن) pointing out that tuhr itself is not evidence of need; rather, the act of initiating divorce in tuhr indicates need. The difference between cause (sabab) and evidence (dalil) is that a cause always has some effect, while evidence may be merely indicative without causal effect. The author notes that only a few examples are given, but many disputed rulings depend on this principle.

Key Terms
إقامة الشيء مقام غيره (iqamat al-shay' maqam ghayrihi): Establishing a thing in place of another; replacing the real effective factor with its cause or evidence.
السبب الداعي (al-sabab al-da'i): The motivating cause; the observable factor that typically leads to an internal state.
الدليل (al-dalil): Evidence that indicates the existence of an internal state or reality.
الطهر (al-tuhr): State of purity from menstruation and sexual intercourse, used as evidence of need for divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between a cause (sabab) and evidence (dalil) according to the passage?
A) A cause always has effect, while evidence may only indicate without effect.
B) Evidence is stronger than a cause.
C) A cause is always internal, while evidence is external.
D) There is no difference; they are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle of 'iqamat al-shay' maqam ghayrihi' allows observable causes and evidence to stand in for internal realities, but the two types differ in that a cause always has effect whereas evidence may only indicate.
